    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="45d86975f8dc4ec8811f487124194b16">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '6c139e2c-36c2-48e2-bce0-8ee84437be9c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:xsun@eng.uwo.ca"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>xsun@eng.uwo.ca</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">
                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a target="_blank" class="sm-account__link" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0003-0374-1245"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-orcid"></i><span>orcid.org/0000-0003-0374-1245</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>Department of Mechanical and Materials Engineering, University of Western Ontario, London, ON, N6A 5B9 Canada</p>
                     <p>Eastern Institute for Advanced Study, Eastern Institute of Technology, Ningbo, Zhejiang, 315200 P. R. China</p>
                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:m.danny.gu@gmail.com"><span>m.danny.gu@gmail.com</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:tsham@uwo.ca"><span>tsham@uwo.ca</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:yfmo@umd.edu"><span>yfmo@umd.edu</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:xsun@eng.uwo.ca"><span>xsun@eng.uwo.ca</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Sun/Xueliang">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d119643556" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The development of solid-state sodium-ion batteries (SSSBs) heavily hinges on the development of an superionic Na<sup>+</sup> conductor (SSC) that features high conductivity, (electro)chemical stability, and deformability. The construction of heterogeneous structures offers a promising approach to comprehensively enhancing these properties in a way that differs from traditional structural optimization. Here, this work exploits the structural variance between high- and low-coordination halide frameworks to develop a new class of halide heterogeneous structure electrolytes (HSEs). The halide HSEs incorporating a UCl<sub>3</sub>-type high-coordination framework and amorphous low-coordination phase achieves the highest Na<sup>+</sup> conductivity (2.7 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at room temperature, RT) among halide SSCs so far. By discerning the individual contribution of the crystalline bulk, amorphous region, and interface, this work unravels the synergistic ion conduction within halide HSEs and provides a comprehensive explanation of the amorphization effect. More importantly, the excellent deformability, high-voltage stability, and expandability of HSEs enable effective SSSB integration. Using a cold-pressed cathode electrode composite of uncoated Na<sub>0.85</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and HSEs, the SSSBs present stable cycle performance with a capacity retention of 91.0% after 100 cycles at 0.2 C.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202308012-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202308012-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Given the growing scarcity of lithium resources, solid-state sodium-ion batteries (SSSBs) have garnered significant attention owing to their sustainable composition, high theoretical energy density, and inherent safety advantages.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0001_R_d119643544e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Sustainability Res. Nat. Rev. Mater. Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the development of solid-state sodium-ion batteries (SSSBs) imposes stringent requirements on superionic Na-ion conductors (SSCs), including high ionic conductivity (&gt;1 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>) at room temperature (RT), excellent deformability for intimate solid-solid contact, and exceptional interfacial stability with electrode materials, yet no single SSC currently fulfills all these requirements simultaneously.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0002_R_d119643544e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - ACS Energy Lett. Nat. Commun. Joule">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the several categories of SSCs, inorganic oxide SSCs<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0003_R_d119643544e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Inorg. Nucl. Chem. Mater. Res. Bull.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> show higher Na-ion conductivity of ≈1 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> and better electrochemical stability than those of polymer-based SSCs, but their rigid nature makes them extremely challenging to maintain tight interparticle contact in SSSBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0001_R_d119643544e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Sustainability Res. Nat. Rev. Mater. Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although closo-borates,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0004_R_d119643544e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ChemSusChem">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> sodium thiophosphates,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0005_R_d119643544e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Commun. J. Solid State Chem.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and their analogues<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0006_R_d119643544e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl. Chem. Mater. Chem. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are emerging in recent years due to their deformability and exceptional ionic conductivity (up to the level of 10 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>), the limited oxidative stability hinders their integration with high-voltage cathodes for competitive energy densities.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, the successful discovery of a range of halide-based&nbsp;Li-ion conductors,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0007_R_d119643544e703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Nat. Commun. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which exhibit good deformability, high ionic conductivity, and (electro)chemical stability, has inspired research into sodium halide analogues as potential SSCs. However, due to the disparity in ionic radius between Li (76 pm) and Na (&gt;100 pm), the design principles for superionic lithium conductors cannot be directly applied to SSCs. As a result, the reported halide-based SSCs generally exhibit insufficient ionic conductivity. For instance, the trigonal structured Na<sub>2</sub>ZrCl<sub>6</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0008_R_d119643544e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Storage Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and its aliovalently substituted Na<sub>3-x</sub>Er<sub>1-x</sub>Zr<sub>x</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0009_R_d119643544e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na<sub>3-x</sub>Y<sub>1-x</sub>Zr<sub>x</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0010_R_d119643544e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Commun.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> present Na<sup>+</sup> conductivities of 0.018 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> (30 °C), 0.04 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> (25 °C), and 0.066 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> (20 °C), respectively. Ridley et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0011_R_d119643544e751" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ChemRxiv. Cambridge: Cambridge Open Engage">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> further demonstrated an increased RT ionic conductivity of Na<sub>x</sub>Y<sub>0.25</sub>Zr<sub>0.75</sub>Cl<sub>3.75+x</sub> to 0.4 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> by reducing the NaCl content and transitioning to an amorphous state. The authors suggest that the bigger free volume and higher number of Na<sup>+</sup> vacancies in the amorphous state likely account for the high ionic conductivity. However, no halide SSC has achieved an ionic conductivity above 1 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> as comparable to the lithium analogues.</p>
         
         <p>The construction of heterogeneous structures offers an alternative approach to enhancing ion conduction distinct from traditional structural optimization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0012_R_d119643544e775" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Rev.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hiram Kwak et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0013_R_d119643544e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> recently reported an intriguing finding of improving the Na<sup>+</sup> conductivity of 0.011 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> for Na<sub>2</sub>ZrCl<sub>6</sub> to 0.11 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> for ZrO<sub>2</sub>-Na<sub>2</sub>ZrCl<sub>6</sub> even if the bulk ZrO<sub>2</sub> is not conductive. Similarly, an increase in ionic conductivity by one to two orders of magnitude has frequently been observed in various heterostructure systems, such as crystal-crystal (LiI·Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0014_R_d119643544e809" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> LiI·Li<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0015_R_d119643544e819" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Storage Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Li<sub>2</sub>ZrCl<sub>6</sub>·ZrO<sub>2</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0013_R_d119643544e830" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>), crystal-glass (AgI·Ag<sub>3</sub>BO<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0016_R_d119643544e839" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Non-Cryst. Solids">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> P<sub>2</sub>S<sub>5</sub>·Li<sub>2</sub>S·LiI,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0017_R_d119643544e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Solid State Ionics">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and LiBH<sub>4</sub>·fumed silica<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0018_R_d119643544e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>), and polymer-inorganic (PEO·Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0019_R_d119643544e867" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Electrochim. Acta">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and PEO·Li<sub>7</sub>La<sub>3</sub>Zr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>12</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0020_R_d119643544e880" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>), which are usually attributed to the effect of space charge layer at the interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0012_R_d119643544e886" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Rev.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the exploration of halide-based heterogeneous structure electrolytes (HSE) as potential SSCs is still on the way, and there remains a lack of clarity regarding the specific roles of heterogeneous components on ionic conductivity.</p>
         
         <p>Besides the common close-packed anion structures of Li<sup>+</sup> superionic conductors, we have lately proposed another group of UCl<sub>3</sub>-type frameworks,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0021_R_d119643544e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in which highly distorted octahedral sites constitutes a fast one-dimensional (1D) diffusion channel for Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> hopping. However, the limitation of 1D transport characteristics restricts its macroscopic ionic conductivity across mismatched grains. Yin et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0022_R_d119643544e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nature">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> take advantage of high-valence cationic doping to obtain three-dimensional (3D) transport within the single structure, leading to a high Li<sup>+</sup> ionic conductivity of 3.02 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at 30 °C. When the UCl<sub>3</sub>-type lattice maintains the fast 1D channels without aliovalent substitution, we found that the UCl<sub>3</sub>-type lattice possesses an appealing capability to induce amorphization in surrounding low-coordination halides, creating interparticle conduction buffers to achieve fast macroscopic ion diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0021_R_d119643544e922" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These behaviors fulfill the key focuses of HSEs development, including the sustained structural integrity of each component and fast ion diffusion in the different components. Hence, incorporating UCl<sub>3</sub>-type frameworks into halide HSEs represents a promising strategy for effectively exploiting the rapid 1D diffusion and amorphization capabilities inherent to the UCl<sub>3</sub>-type framework, thereby enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> ion conductivity synergistically.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we delve into the structural characteristics of polycationic halides that incorporate the UCl<sub>3</sub>-type frameworks and different ternary metal halides. By exploiting the structural variances between high- and low-coordination halide frameworks, we have developed a novel class of halide HSEs that exhibit an outstanding ionic conductivity of up to 2.7 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>. This achievement marks the highest value recorded among halide SSCs to date. The heterogeneous interactions within the HSEs were characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD), pair distribution function (PDF), high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM), and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS). By combining ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations with low-temperature electrochemical impedance fittings, we clarified the individual contribution of the crystalline bulk, amorphous region, and interface to ionic conductivity. Notably, we distinctly highlighted the substantial role of the amorphization effect in this context. Furthermore, the halide HSEs demonstrated excellent deformability, high-voltage stability, and expandability. The cold-pressed cathode composite using uncoated Na<sub>0.85</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMNFO) and HSEs presented stable cycle performance with a capacity retention of 91.0% after 100 cycles at 0.2 C in SSSBs, outperforming the composite performance using Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> as catholyte. The composition of halide HSEs is highly designable and extendable, allowing for a tunable cathode–electrolyte interphase (CEI) design for different cathode materials and requirements.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202308012-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202308012-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202308012-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202308012-sec-0030-title">2.1 Construction and Characterization of Halide Heterostructures</h3>
            
            <p>Two types of sodium metal ternary halides, categorized based on the distinct metal-halogen coordination numbers (C.N.), were mechanochemically synthesized in this study: 1) high coordination frameworks (HCF, C.N. &gt; 6) represented by UCl<sub>3</sub>-type Na<sub>3x</sub>M<sub>2-x</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub> (M = La, Sm) and 2) low coordination frameworks (LCF, C.N. ≤ 6) represented by dispersed [MCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>x−</sup> octahedra, such as NaTaCl<sub>6</sub> (LCF-Ta). <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0001">1a,b</a> presents the Rietveld refinements of XRD patterns of the HCF candidates, Na<sub>0.75</sub>Sm<sub>1.75</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub> (HCF-Sm) and Na<sub>0.75</sub>La<sub>1.75</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub> (HCF-La). HCF-Sm and HCF-La are constituted by [MCl<sub>9</sub>]<sup>6−</sup> tricapped trigonal prism with Na atoms partially occupying Wyckoff sites <i>2b</i> (octahedral sites) and <i>2c</i> (La, Sm sites), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0001">1d</a>. In the UCl<sub>3</sub>-type structure, the AIMD simulations revealed fast Na<sup>+</sup> conduction in the numerous face-sharing distorted octahedra sites along the <i>c</i>-axis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a,b</a>, Supporting Information). As for an LCF candidate, according to the Rietveld refinements of XRD pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0001">1c</a>), the crystalline LCF-Ta comprises an [TaCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup> octahedral framework located at the corners of the unit cell (<i>P</i>2<sub>1</sub>/c) edge-sharing by two [NaCl<sub>7</sub>]<sup>6−</sup> capped trigonal prism (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0001">1e</a>). Different from the UCl<sub>3</sub>-type structure, Na<sup>+</sup> ions in LCF-Ta are confined at equilibrium sites surrounding by orderly distributed [TaCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup> octahedra as depicted in AIMD simulations (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a>, Supporting Information). The formation of a 3D diffusional network in LCF-Ta requires overcoming a substantial activation energy barrier, which gives rise to an experimental Na<sup>+</sup> conductivity of 5.0 × 10<sup>−2</sup> mS cm<sup>−1</sup>. In contrast, the 1D diffusion of HCF-Sm and HCF-La hinders macroscopic percolation due to lattice mismatching between polycrystalline and results in low experimental Na<sup>+</sup> conductivities of 1.1 × 10<sup>−4</sup> and 5.5 × 10<sup>−5</sup> mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at RT (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1d–f</a>, Supporting Information), respectively. Both types of Na-based halide materials currently have their limitations, leading to low apparent ionic conductivities. Following Pauling's rules for ionic compounds, the cation–anion radius ratio r<sup>+</sup>/r<sup>−</sup> determines the C.N. of the cation and the stability of the cation within the coordinated polyhedron of anions. Therefore, the dissolution of [SmCl<sub>9</sub>]<sup>6−</sup> and [TaCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup> into each other becomes challenging. A combination of the two shall likely form a heterogeneous structure rather than a solid solution, thus fully utilizing the fast 1D Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in UCl<sub>3</sub>-type HCFs and their capability of inducing amorphization.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202308012-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/83893b29-e961-447c-b2b8-326f153458ee/adma202308012-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/83893b29-e961-447c-b2b8-326f153458ee/adma202308012-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/96f74b4c-9b84-4771-9820-30076d2dbff0/adma202308012-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/83893b29-e961-447c-b2b8-326f153458ee/adma202308012-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202308012-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202308012" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of HCF-Sm/La, LCF-Ta, and their heterogeneous composite. Synchrotron-based X-ray diffraction patterns (SXRD, <i>λ</i> = 0.3497 Å) and the Rietveld refinements of a) Na<sub>0.75</sub>Sm<sub>1.75</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub> (HCF-Sm), b) Na<sub>0.75</sub>La<sub>1.75</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub> (HCF-La), and c) NaTaCl<sub>6</sub> (LCF-Ta). d) The crystal structure of UCl<sub>3</sub>-type Na<sub>0.75</sub>M<sub>1.75</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub> (M = La, Sm). e) The crystal structure of NaTaCl<sub>6</sub>. The XRD patterns (<i>λ</i> = 1.5406 Å, Cu K<sub>α</sub>) of composites of LCF-Ta with f) HCF-Sm as (1-x)[HCF-Sm]·x[LCF-Ta] and g) HCF-La as (1-x)[HCF-La]·x[LCF-Ta]. h) Arrhenius plots of the LCF-Ta, 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta], and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta]. The ionic conductivities of i) (1 − x)[HCF-Sm]·x[LCF-Ta] and j) (1 − x)[HCF-La]·x[LCF-Ta] at 25 °C. k) The ionic conductivities of HSEs based on 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38LCF and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43LCF using different LCFs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The optimal composition and synthesis conditions of halide HSEs between HCF and LCF were investigated. The diffraction patterns of the heterostructures are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0001">1f,g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). As the LCF-Ta content decreases, the diffraction peaks become weaker, leading to a noticeable increase in the dominance of the HCF-Sm/HCF-La diffraction peaks. The resulting HSEs exhibit enhanced ionic conductivities (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0001">1h</a>) and low activation energies (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) compared with those of the individual LCF-Ta sample. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0001">1i,j</a>, both in (1-<i>x</i>)[HCF-Sm]·<i>x</i>[LCF-Ta] and (1‒<i>x</i>)[HCF-La]·<i>x</i>[LCF-Ta], the ionic conductivity is optimized within the range of <i>x</i> = 0.35‒0.5. Notably, the composition of 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] demonstrates an exceptional ionic conductivity of 2.7 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at 25 °C, while 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] exhibits a comparable ionic conductivity of 1.8 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at 25 °C. These values represent the highest conductivities achieved to date compared to that of other reported halide SSCs (0.02–0.11 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>). In addition, as proof of the generality of the halide HSE design, LCF-Ta was further replaced with Na<sub>2</sub>ZrCl<sub>6</sub> (LCF-Zr) or Na<sub>2</sub>HfCl<sub>6</sub> (LCF-Hf), respectively. The corresponding ionic conductivity also increased from the level of 0.01 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> (pristine Na<sub>2</sub>ZrCl<sub>6</sub> or Na<sub>2</sub>HfCl<sub>6</sub>) to the level of 0.1 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> (for HSEs) as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0001">1k</a>. The SXRD observed similar sharp peaks from HCF-Sm/HCF-La and large bumps around 2<i>θ</i> = 4° (<i>λ</i> = 0.3497 Å) from LCF-Zr/LCF-Hf (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a,b</a>, Supporting Information), confirming the formation of heterostructures.</p>
            
            <p>To investigate the structural characteristics of HSEs, we conducted SXRD and PDF measurements on 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta], as depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0002">2a,b</a>. After heterogenized and synthesis of HSEs, the diffraction peaks relevant to LCF-Ta seem greatly quenched. Indeed, the crystalline LCF-Ta transforms into an amorphous state instead of forming a solid solution with HCF-Sm or HCF-La. The distribution of distances <i>G</i>(r) between pairs of atoms in 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] can be found in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0002">2c</a>. The peak at approximately 2.4 Å characterizes the Ta-Cl distance in octahedron of LCF-Ta, and the peak at ≈2.8‒2.9 Å characterizes the Sm/La-Cl distance in tricapped trigonal prisms of HCF-Sm/La. Both 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] present a mixed characteristic of [TaCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup> octahedron and [MCl<sub>9</sub>]<sup>6−</sup> tricapped trigonal prisms (M = La, Sm), which confirms that 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] are a composite of crystalline HCF-Sm/HCF-La and amorphous LCF-Ta. Similarly, [ZrCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>2−</sup> or [HfCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>2−</sup> octahedrons have been identified in the PDF of HSEs using LCF-Zr or LCF-Hf (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4c,d</a>, Supporting Information). The “box-car fitting” was utilized to extract quantitative information about the length scale of local atomic order from the PDF analysis of complex materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0023_R_d119643544e1247" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Examination of the PDF through a box-car fitting method (box length of 6 Å, where the centroid of the refinement range is shifted to higher <i>r</i> in 1 Å steps) showed that fitted ratio of LCF-Ta in 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] changed as a function of fitting length scale. The ratio of LCF-Ta rapidly declines at around <i>r</i><sub>max</sub> = 9 Å and approaches ≈0 at <i>r</i><sub>max</sub> = 18 Å, which confirms the amorphization of LCF-Ta.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202308012-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bbff956b-fa87-44cd-ad72-69144a1da4c4/adma202308012-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bbff956b-fa87-44cd-ad72-69144a1da4c4/adma202308012-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d28aa8a9-13e6-4074-9526-ad0e185546d6/adma202308012-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bbff956b-fa87-44cd-ad72-69144a1da4c4/adma202308012-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202308012-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202308012" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Crystalline-amorphous heterogeneous structures and enhanced ionic conductivities. a) The SXRD patterns&nbsp;(<i>λ</i> = 0.3497 Å) and b) PDFs (<i>λ</i> = 0.1905 Å) of LCF-Ta, HCF-La, HCF-Sm, 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta], and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta]. c) The fitted mass ratio of LCF-Ta in 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta], and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] verse <i>r</i><sub>max</sub> from the box-car fitting method. d,e) The HRTEM image taken from the particle edge of 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta]. f) The electrochemical impedance plot of 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] at −70 °C. g) The schematic diagram of heterogeneous halide composites.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The crystalline-amorphous heterostructure of 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] particles was further confirmed by HRTEM analysis. The HRTEM image of 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0002">2d,e</a>) clearly shows several grains that are spread throughout the particle and interconnected by the amorphous region. Combined XRD and the selected area electron diffraction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information) results, the crystalline regions were identified as HCF-Sm, and its (020) and (011) planes were indicated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0002">2d</a>. Moreover, the corresponding energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping on the whole particle (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrates a uniform distribution of Na and Cl elements but a slightly localized distribution of Sm and Ta elements, which further supports that the 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] sample is a mixture rather than a solid solution.</p>
            
            <p>To distinguish the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion of different components, we studied the low-temperature electrochemical impedances of 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta], HCF-Sm, and LCF-Ta. The LCF-Ta presents a symmetric impedance semicircle (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a>, Supporting Information) at −70&nbsp;°C, whereas both HCF-Sm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a>, Supporting Information, 5 °C) and 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0002">2f</a>, −70 °C) present an asymmetric impedance semicircle. By fitting the impedance result of 0.62 HCF-Sm·0.38 LCF-Ta, the origins of ionic conductivity were differentiated as the bulk-phase HCF-Sm, amorphous LCF-Ta, and interface/grain boundaries according to the capacitances from low to high, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0002">2g</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0024_R_d119643544e1322" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The ionic diffusions in these three regions exhibit a remarkable enhancement when compared to those observed in pristine HCF-Sm or LCF-Ta. Thus, the study of synergistic ion conduction among the crystalline region, the amorphous region, and the interface between crystalline and amorphous warrants thorough investigation.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202308012-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202308012-sec-0040-title">2.2 The Synergistic Effect Enabled by Heterogenization</h3>
            
            <p>To decipher the ion transport in the complex system of HSEs, we began by individually considering the impacts of the crystalline region, the amorphous region, and the interface. Previous studies have examined the space charge layer effect at interfaces<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0012_R_d119643544e1336" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Rev.">12</a>, <a href="#adma202308012-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0025_R_d119643544e1336" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Solid State Ionics J. Electrochem. Soc. Nature">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the diffusion mechanism in UCl<sub>3</sub>-type frameworks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0021_R_d119643544e1343" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a>, <a href="#adma202308012-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0022_R_d119643544e1343" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nature">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, we employ simulations to evaluate the influence of amorphous LCF-Ta on Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. To generate the amorphous structure, we conduct thermal quenching of the crystalline LCF-Ta at temperatures of 1200 and 2000 K and obtain two amorphous structures with increased disordering (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0003">3a-c</a>). While Ta-Cl remains local octahedral configurations in the amorphous structures, a notable change in Ta cation ordering is observed in amorphous LCF-Ta, leading to the diverse local environment of Na<sup>+</sup> sites (as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The disordering of the [TaCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup> framework was further illustrated by the calculated radial distribution functions as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0004">4a,b</a>, and the continuous symmetry measure (CSM) analysis suggests stronger distortion of the [TaCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup> octahedrons in amorphous LCF-Ta (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0004">4c</a>, 2000&nbsp;K-quenched &gt;1200K-quenched &gt; Pristine), which is correlated with ionic conductivity as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0003">3e</a>. According to the mechanism of ion diffusion in frustration as illustrated,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0026_R_d119643544e1384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the Na<sup>+</sup> hopping between these disordered sites has reduced barriers, thus enhancing the ionic conductivity as shown in AIMD simulations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0003">3e</a>, 2000 K-quenched &gt; 1200 K-quenched &gt; Pristine).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202308012-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/218a266b-3327-479d-9729-086de5ef45ee/adma202308012-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/218a266b-3327-479d-9729-086de5ef45ee/adma202308012-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/340651f3-77cf-4922-9fe2-5327ae6a4022/adma202308012-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/218a266b-3327-479d-9729-086de5ef45ee/adma202308012-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202308012-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202308012" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The effect of amorphization of LCF-Ta. Below is the Na<sup>+</sup> probability density marked by yellow isosurfaces from AIMD simulations at 700 K. d) Schematics of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in crystal versus amorphized LCF-Ta.                   </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202308012-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/23190528-589c-4b8e-a70e-8bcb3212ad79/adma202308012-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/23190528-589c-4b8e-a70e-8bcb3212ad79/adma202308012-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0dfc9a5d-ee4e-4466-8de1-4312c4e87748/adma202308012-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/23190528-589c-4b8e-a70e-8bcb3212ad79/adma202308012-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202308012-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202308012" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Local distortion in the amorphous LCF-Ta. Calculated radial distribution functions of a) Ta-Cl distance and b) Ta–Ta distance of LCF-Ta. c) The CSM of Ta–Cl Octahedra in LCF-Ta. For each box, the centerline marks the median of the data set, the center square marks the mean of the data set, the outer edges mark the first and third quartiles, and the endpoints are either the extrema values or 1.5 times the interquartile range, whichever is smaller. Data are marked individually as diamonds. All the configurations are sampled at constant 2 ps intervals from the snapshots in AIMD simulation at 600&nbsp;K. d) Fourier transform of the Ta L<sub>3</sub>-edge EXAFS in R-space, with a <i>k</i><sup>2</sup>-weighing, of LCF-Ta, 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta], and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta]. e,f) The schematic of the relationship between bond angle and the intensity of multiple scattering. g) The Ta L<sub>3</sub>-edge EXAFS region in k-space of LCF-Ta, 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta], and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta]. Wavelet transform of the <i>k</i><sup>2</sup>-weighted EXAFS data of h) LCF-Ta and i) 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta].</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>EXAFS is a technique that enables the analysis of local structural information around a specific element absorber, making it an ideal tool for investigating the local coordination of specific elements in complex systems. The Fourier-transformed (FT) <i>k<sup>2</sup></i>-weighted χ(<i>k</i>) of Ta <i>L<sub>3</sub></i>-edge EXAFS spectra of LCF-Ta, 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta], and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] are present in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0004">4d</a>. The backscattering signals from the first shell of Ta atoms settle at <i>R</i> = 2.4 Å (after phase correction) in LCF-Ta, 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta], and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta], consistent with the Ta-Cl distance obtained from the PDF analysis. Similarly, the Sm <i>L<sub>3</sub></i>-edge EXAFS spectra of 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] demonstrate only one characteristic peak located at <i>R</i> = 2.8 Å in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8d</a> (Supporting Information), which is identical to that of HCF-Sm and should be ascribed to the backscattering from Cl<sup>−</sup> coordination in [SmCl<sub>9</sub>]<sup>6−</sup> tricapped trigonal prisms. This excludes the probability that Ta and Sm are partially displacing each other in the mixture of LCF-Ta and HCF-Sm. Further, in the multiple-scattering region of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0004">4d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), the focused hinge paths (Ta-Cl-Ta-Cl-Ta at <i>R</i> &gt; 4 Å) are very sensitive to the bond angle of Cl-Ta-Cl as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0004">4e,f</a> (the closer to 180°, the stronger). After further differentiation of scattering signs in <i>k</i>-space (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0004">4g-i</a>), the peak intensity of <i>f1</i> at <i>k</i> = 6 Å<sup>−1</sup> and <i>R</i> = 4.2 Å can be viewed as a criterion of distortion of [TaCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup> octahedrons. Following the amorphization of LCF-Ta in the heterostructure, we observe a notable increase in the distortion of [TaCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup> octahedrons. This distortion causes a deviation of the central Ta atom, as evidenced by the decrease in the intensity of <i>f1</i> in the heterostructure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0004">4i</a>) compared to pristine LCF-Ta (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0004">4h</a>). The distorting tendency strongly corroborates with our computational simulations, revealing notable alterations in the local environment of Ta within LCF-Ta.</p>
            
            <p>Based on the analysis of the amorphous region, the interplay between the three regions in halide HSEs can be summarized as follows: the LCF-Ta transitions into an amorphous state characterized by disordered [TaCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup> octahedrons when forming the heterogeneous interface with the UCl<sub>3</sub>-type framework (i.e., HCFs); the disorder of [TaCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup> octahedrons leads to the diverse local environments around the Na<sup>+</sup> sites with enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> ionic conductivity. These inter-grain amorphous regions then play a critical role in connecting different UCl<sub>3</sub>-type frameworks, enabling long-range Na<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;conduction across the HCF grains and the amorphous LCF&nbsp;regions. The heterogeneous interface makes a substantial contribution to the overall ionic conductivity. However, comprehending and simulating this interface presents significant challenges, which will be the focus of our future research. Overall, the three regions synergistically enhanced the ionic conduction via the heterogenization of halide HSEs.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202308012-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202308012-sec-0050-title">2.3 The SSSB Performance Using Halide HSEs</h3>
            
            <p>In addition to their excellent ionic conductivity compared to other halide electrolytes, halide HSEs also possess favorable properties such as deformability and exceptional high-voltage stability. The deformability of solid electrolytes (SEs) can be directly assessed by examining the relative porosity of cold-pressed pellets under a specific pressure. We pressed the electrolyte powders into a pellet under ≈400 MPa. The cross-sectional morphologies of these pellets were examined by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). As depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), pores and voids are clearly observed in high-magnification images of Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> but absent in halide HSEs. The estimated relative density of 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta], 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta], and Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> from SEM results are 92.2%, 89.4%, and 75.2%, respectively, indicating that HSEs has better processibility than the typical sulfide SSC of Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub>.</p>
            
            <p>The thermodynamic electrochemical windows (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) of HCF-Sm, HCF-La, LCF-Ta, and Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> were calculated using the grand potential phase diagram approach (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0005">5a</a>). The stable electrochemical windows were determined as 0.64‒4.25 V for HCF-Sm and 0.41‒3.76 V for HCF-La, respectively. LCF-Ta has a similar oxidation limit of 3.91 V but a high reduction limit of 2.15 V due to the reduction of Ta<sup>5+</sup>. Overall, halide SSCs generally demonstrate higher oxidation limits than sulfides, such as Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> (1.21 – 2.12 V). The experimental electrochemical windows of 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] are measured using linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) analyses. Matching with theoretical prediction results, 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] has a higher oxidation limit than that of 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202308012-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/48eb8dda-d73f-495a-befa-89beef51f878/adma202308012-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/48eb8dda-d73f-495a-befa-89beef51f878/adma202308012-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/03e0735b-7113-426b-93d9-eea4aad857c8/adma202308012-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/48eb8dda-d73f-495a-befa-89beef51f878/adma202308012-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202308012-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202308012" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical stability and battery performance. a) The calculated stable electrochemical window of different SEs. b) Thermodynamic calculation of decomposition energies between SEs and cathode materials at sodiated and de-sodiated states. c) The 1<sup>st</sup> charge/discharge profile of cells in which 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta], 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta], or Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> is used as catholyte at RT and 0.1 C (1&nbsp;C = 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). d,e) the rate performance of cells using 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] as catholyte at RT. f) The charge/discharge curves of the cell in (d) at various current densities. g) The long cycle performance of cycled between 2.3–4 V vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at 0.2 C at 25 °C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The chemical andelectrochemical stabilities between LCF-Ta and other SSCs and various cathode materials were further evaluated by computation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0027_R_d119643544e1635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Mater. Chem. ACS Energy Lett.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0005">5b</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5–S8</a> (Supporting Information) depict the reaction energy and decomposition products between SSCs and cathode materials including NMNFO, NaCrO<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. The de-sodiation of these three cathode materials notably results in instability in contact with Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub>. Conversely, halide SSCs show greater stability as the Na content decreases. As the options of metal elements for halide HSEs are versatile (e.g., Sm/La and Ta/Zr/Hf) without significantly compromising the high ionic conductivity of the electrolyte, the electrochemical window of halide HSEs and composition of CEI are tunable.</p>
            
            <p>As a proof of concept, the compatibility between different halide HSEs and cathode active materials was studied in SSSBs. A Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> interlayer was inserted between the HSEs and the Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> alloy anode. NMNFO was selected as a representative cathode material due to its appropriate operating voltage up to 4 V. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0005">5c</a> presents the initial charge–discharge curves of NMNFO-based SSSBs using different electrolytes at 0.1 C (1 C = 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). As a control experiment, the cell using only Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> as the electrolyte showed little discharge capacity after being charged to 4 V. In sharp contrast, the cells using either 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] or 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] exhibited a reversible charge–discharge process. The interfacial reaction between different electrolytes and NMNFO was investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). As indicated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a,b</a> (Supporting Information) for the cell using Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub>, both S <i>2p</i> and P <i>2p</i> XPS spectra exhibited strong signals from P<sub>2</sub>S<sub>5</sub> and bridging sulfur (P-[S]<sub>n</sub>-P), indicating a severe decomposition of Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> at 4 V, and this result is consistent with previous reports on electrodes using Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0008_R_d119643544e1709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Storage Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the cells using halide HSEs, marginal changes of Sm <i>3d</i>, La <i>3d</i>, Ta <i>4f</i>, and Cl <i>2p</i> spectra were observed after being charged to 4 V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c–h</a>, Supporting Information), confirming the exceptional cationic stability of 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] compared to that of Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub>. As we have exhibited above, the electrochemical performance of HSEs is far superior to that of Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> when charging the cell to 4 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
            
            <p>The rate and cycle performance of cells using 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] or 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] are comparable, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202308012-fig-0005">5d–g</a>. Specifically, the 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] cell showed capacity retention of 91.0% after 100 cycles at 0.2 C, which is slightly higher than the 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] cell (88.3%). To be noted, the observed performance at high rates is mainly limited by the poor ionic conductivity of the interlayer Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> (≈0.1 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>). While the ionic conductivities of 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] are comparable, the discharge capacity and Coulombic efficiency (CE) in the initial cycle is higher when using 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] than using 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] (≈117.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> vs. ≈95.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>; 96.1% vs. 85.8%). This can be attributed to the higher oxidation limit of 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] due to the use of HCF-Sm instead of HCF-La. Recent research has shown the critical role of metal elements in halide electrolytes in determining the high-voltage stability of the battery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0028_R_d119643544e1755" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Environ. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Within HSEs, the choice of components also affects the performance of the battery as the 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] cell delivered higher discharge capacity whereas the 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] cell exhibited better cycle performance.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202308012-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202308012-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, our findings lead to a novel class of halide HSEs by utilizing the synergistic effect between high- and low-coordination halide frameworks, demonstrating comprehensive advantages of high ionic conductivity, excellent high-voltage stability, and deformability. The optimized 0.62[HCF-Sm]·0.38[LCF-Ta] and 0.57[HCF-La]·0.43[LCF-Ta] HSEs exhibited a remarkable RT Na<sup>+</sup> conductivity of 2.7 and 1.8 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are among the highest values reported so far for halide SSCs. The diffusion mechanism in this class of halide HSEs was studied, revealing distinct contributions from crystalline bulk, amorphous region, and the heterogeneous interface between them. In particular, the effect of amorphized components in HSEs was studied. The amorphization process significantly increases the ionic conductivity of target components and assists in establishing connections between discrete UCl<sub>3</sub>-type framework particles, thereby enabling fast long-range Na<sup>+</sup> conduction across the macroscopic HSEs. With good deformability and high-voltage stability of HSEs, direct integration with NMNFO cathode can be achieved by simple cold-pressing to demonstrate a stable cathode/catholyte interface and a capacity retention of 91.0% after 100 cycles at 0.2 C for SSSBs. In addition, a theoretical study of interfacial reactions between SSCs and cathode materials revealed different interfacial products upon the choice of SSCs and cathode materials. The component in HSEs is versatile, allowing for a tunable CEI design for different cathode materials. Owing to the comprehensive advantages, the heterogeneous halide composite SSC is an ideal choice for achieving high-performance SSSBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202308012-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202308012-sec-0070-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202308012-sec-0080">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202308012-sec-0090">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202308012-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202308012-sec-0100-title"> DFT Calculation</h4>
                  
                  <p>All density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed using Vienna Ab initio Simulation package (VASP)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0029_R_d119643544e1796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Phys. Rev. B">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> within the projector augmented-wave (PAW) approach<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0030_R_d119643544e1801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Phys. Rev. B">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) generalized-gradient approximation (GGA) functional.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0031_R_d119643544e1806" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Chem. Phys.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The convergence parameters in static DFT calculations were consistent with the Materials Project.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0032_R_d119643544e1812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - APL Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202308012-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202308012-sec-0110-title"> Generation of Amorphous Structures</h4>
                  
                  <p>The supercell models of crystal NaTaCl<sub>6</sub> (a total of 128 atoms) were heated from 100 K to target temperature (1200 and 2000 K) at a constant rate within a period of 2 ps and then equilibrated for 20 ps. Finally, the configurations were quenched to 100&nbsp;K at a constant rate within a period of 2 ps. In all simulations, non-spin-polarized mode, a time step of 2 fs, and NVT ensemble using a Nose´-Hoover thermostat<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0033_R_d119643544e1827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Prog. Theor. Phys. Suppl.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> were used.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202308012-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202308012-sec-0120-title"> Ab Initio Molecular Dynamics (AIMD) Simulations</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">To investigate ionic diffusion, AIMD simulations were performed on the supercell models of HCF-SM (a total of 68 atoms), HCF-LA (a total of 68 atoms), and LCF-TA (a total of 128 atoms). Non-spin-polarized mode and a Γ-centered k-point were used. A time step of 2 fs and NVT ensemble using a Nose´-Hoover thermostat<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0033_R_d119643544e1841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Prog. Theor. Phys. Suppl.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was used in all simulations. The structures were heated from 100 K to the targeted temperature (400–1000 K) at a constant rate during a period of 2 ps. The <i>D</i> was calculated as the mean square displacement (MSD) over the time interval Δ<i>t</i>:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202308012-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                                          <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                          <mo>=</mo>
                                          <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                          <mn>1</mn>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mi>N</mi>
                                    </msubsup>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mfenced close="〉" open="〈" separators="">
                                          <msup>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mrow>
                                                      <mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
                                                      <mi mathvariant="normal">r</mi>
                                                   </mrow>
                                                   <mi>i</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                                <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                                   <mrow>
                                                      <mi>t</mi>
                                                      <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                                      <mo>+</mo>
                                                      <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                                      <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                                      <mi>t</mi>
                                                   </mrow>
                                                </mfenced>
                                                <mo>−</mo>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi mathvariant="normal">r</mi>
                                                   <mi>i</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                                <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                                                   <mi>t</mi>
                                                </mfenced>
                                                <mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
                                             </mrow>
                                             <mn>2</mn>
                                          </msup>
                                       </mfenced>
                                       <mi>t</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}D{\mathrm{\ = \ }}\frac{{\mathrm{1}}}{{{{2Nd\Delta t}}}}\sum\nolimits_{{{i\ = \ 1}}}^{{N}} {{{\left\langle {{{{\mathrm{[r}}_{{i}}\left( {{{t\ + \ \Delta t}}} \right){\mathrm{ - }}{{\mathrm{r}}}_{{i}}\left( {{t}} \right){\mathrm{]}}}}^{\mathrm{2}}} \right\rangle }}_{{t}}} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
                  </div>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The ionic conductivity was calculated according to the Nernst–Einstein relationship:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202308012-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a4d61fd5-919d-497c-ab9a-e967c7d12b94/adma202308012-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202308012:adma202308012-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adma202308012-math-0002.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi>σ</mi>
                                       <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                       <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                       <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    </mrow>
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>n</mi>
                                          <msup>
                                             <mi>q</mi>
                                             <mn>2</mn>
                                          </msup>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">k</mi>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                          <mi>T</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                    <mi>D</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{\sigma \ = \ }}\frac{{n{q}^{\mathrm{2}}}}{{{{\mathrm{k}}}_{\mathrm{B}}T}}D\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where <i>n</i> is the mobile ions volume density and <i>q</i> is the ionic charge. The activation energy and ionic conductivity at the desired temperature were extrapolated according to the Arrhenius relation:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202308012-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d2f6b875-0f7f-4841-90f7-2706124bf986/adma202308012-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202308012:adma202308012-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/adma202308012-math-0003.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>σ</mi>
                                    <mi>T</mi>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>σ</mi>
                                       <mn>0</mn>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mi>exp</mi>
                                    <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                       <mfrac>
                                          <mrow>
                                             <mo>−</mo>
                                             <msub>
                                                <mi>E</mi>
                                                <mi>a</mi>
                                             </msub>
                                          </mrow>
                                          <mrow>
                                             <msub>
                                                <mi>k</mi>
                                                <mi>B</mi>
                                             </msub>
                                             <mi>T</mi>
                                          </mrow>
                                       </mfrac>
                                    </mfenced>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\sigma T\ = \ {\sigma }_0\exp \left( {\frac{{ - {E}_a}}{{{k}_BT}}} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> is the activation energy, <i>σ</i><sub>0</sub> is the pre-exponential factor. The statistical deviation of the diffusivity was evaluated using the scheme established in previous work,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0034_R_d119643544e1987" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - npj Comput. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> then the simulations lasted 40 to 300 ps until the diffusivity converged within a relative standard deviation of 20–40%. The Na-ion probability density was calculated as the time fraction of Na-ions at each spatial location in the crystal structures during the AIMD simulations. The demonstration of crystal structure was performed by VESTA software.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202308012-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202308012-bib-0035_R_d119643544e1992" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></div>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202308012-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202308012-sec-0130-title"> Thermodynamic Calculations of Interface Stabilities</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">To evaluate the interfacial stability between SE and common cathode materials, the interface pseudo-binary reaction energy was calculated as:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202308012-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9dfbd447-e471-470f-8bd4-3afb23b31fdc/adma202308012-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202308012:adma202308012-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/adma202308012-math-0004.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mtable displaystyle="true">
                                    <mtr>
                                       <mtd columnalign="right">
                                          <mrow>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                             <msub>
                                                <mi>E</mi>
                                                <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                                             </msub>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mrow>
                                                   <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                                                   <mi>SE</mi>

                                                   <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>

                                                </mrow>
                                                <mi>x</mi>

                                             </mrow>
                                          </mrow>
                                       </mtd>
                                       <mtd></mtd>
                                       <mtd columnalign="left">
                                          <mrow>
                                             <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                             <msub>
                                                <mi>E</mi>
                                                <mi>eq</mi>
                                             </msub>
                                             <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                                <mrow>
                                                   <msub>
                                                      <mi>C</mi>
                                                      <mi>interface</mi>
                                                   </msub>
                                                   <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
